The user is interested in the intersection of three main areas: (1) electrochemical sensor technology, (2) the IDA method (a supramolecular sensing strategy where an indicator is displaced by the analyte, causing a measurable signal change), and (3) the target analyte quinine. The tool analysis confirms that 'indicator_displacement_assay(IDA)' and 'quinine' are highly information-rich classes, suggesting that the focus should be on the use of IDA in sensor platforms for quinine detection, possibly involving supramolecular hosts such as calixarenes.", 'hypothetical_answer': 'Yes, electrochemical sensors utilizing the Indicator Displacement Assay (IDA) have been explored for the detection of quinine, although such applications are less common than optical (colorimetric or fluorescence) IDA sensors. In an IDA-based electrochemical sensor, a host molecule (often a supramolecular receptor such as a calixarene or cyclodextrin) is immobilized on an electrode surface along with an electroactive indicator. In the absence of quinine, the indicator is bound to the host, producing a characteristic electrochemical signal. When quinine is introduced, it competes with the indicator for binding to the host, displacing the indicator and causing a measurable change in the electrochemical response (such as current or potential shift). This displacement forms the basis for selective and sensitive detection of quinine. Calixarenes, in particular, have been used as host molecules due to their ability to form inclusion complexes with quinine, and their integration into electrochemical platforms has been reported. The IDA approach offers advantages such as high selectivity, tunability, and the potential for miniaturization and real-time analysis. However, most IDA-based quinine sensors reported in the literature are optical; electrochemical implementations are emerging and represent a promising direction for future sensor development.', In an IDA-based electrochemical sensor, a host molecule (often a supramolecular receptor such as a calixarene or cyclodextrin) is immobilized on an electrode surface along with an electroactive indicator. In the absence of quinine, the indicator is bound to the host, producing a characteristic electrochemical signal. When quinine is introduced, it competes with the indicator for binding to the host, displacing the indicator and causing a measurable change in the electrochemical response (such as current or potential shift). This displacement forms the basis for selective and sensitive detection of quinine. Calixarenes, in particular, have been used as host molecules due to their ability to form inclusion complexes with quinine, and their integration into electrochemical platforms has been reported. The IDA approach offers advantages such as high selectivity, tunability, and the potential for miniaturization and real-time analysis. However, most IDA-based quinine sensors reported in the literature are optical; electrochemical implementations are emerging and represent a promising direction for future sensor development.', In an IDA-based electrochemical sensor, a host molecule (often a supramolecular receptor such as a calixarene or cyclodextrin) is immobilized on an electrode surface along with an electroactive indicator. In the absence of quinine, the indicator is bound to the host, producing a characteristic electrochemical signal. When quinine is introduced, it competes with the indicator for binding to the host, displacing the indicator and causing a measurable change in the electrochemical response (such as current or potential shift). This displacement forms the basis for selective and sensitive detection of quinine. Calixarenes, in particular, have been used as host molecules due to their ability to form inclusion complexes with quinine, and their integration into electrochemical platforms has been reported. The IDA approach offers advantages such as high selectivity, tunability, and the potential for miniaturization and real-time analysis. However, most IDA-based quinine sensors reported in the literature are optical; electrochemical implementations are emerging and represent a promising direction for future sensor development.', In an IDA-based electrochemical sensor, a host molecule (often a supramolecular receptor such as a calixarene or cyclodextrin) is immobilized on an electrode surface along with an electroactive indicator. In the absence of quinine, the indicator is bound to the host, producing a characteristic electrochemical signal. When quinine is introduced, it competes with the indicator for binding to the host, displacing the indicator and causing a measurable change in the electrochemical response (such as current or potential shift). This displacement forms the basis for selective and sensitive detection of quinine. Calixarenes, in particular, have been used as host molecules due to their ability to form inclusion complexes with quinine, and their integration into electrochemical platforms has been reported. The IDA approach offers advantages such as high selectivity, tunability, and the potential for miniaturization and real-time analysis. However, most IDA-based quinine sensors reported in the literature are optical; electrochemical implementations are emerging and represent a promising direction for future sensor development.',
